A function implementing `like=` must call its\n120:         dispatcher with `like` as the first non-keyword argument.\n121:     module : str, optional\n122:         __module__ attribute to set on new function, e.g., ``module='numpy'``.\n123:         By default, module is copied from the decorated function.\n124:     verify : bool, optional\n125:         If True, verify the that the signature of the dispatcher and decorated\n126:         function signatures match exactly: all required and optional arguments\n127:         should appear in order with the same names, but the default values for\n128:         all optional arguments should be ``None``. This is useful for\n        functions defined in C, which otherwise don't have docstrings.\n\n    Returns\n    -------\n    Function suitable for decorating the implementation of a NumPy function.\n\n    \"\"\"\n    def decorator(implementation):\n        if verify:\n            if dispatcher is not None:\n                verify_matching_signatures(implementation, dispatcher)\n            else:\n                # Using __code__ directly similar to verify_matching_signature\n                co = implementation.__code__\n                last_arg = co.co_argcount + co.co_kwonlyargcount - 1\n                last_arg = co.co_varnames[last_arg]\n                if last_arg != \"like\" or co.co_kwonlyargcount == 0:\n                    raise RuntimeError(\n                        \"__array_function__ expects `like=` to be the last \"\n                        \"argument and a keyword-only argument. \"\n                        f\"{implementation} does not seem to comply.\")\n\n        if docs_from_dispatcher:\n            add_docstring(implementation, dispatcher.__doc__)\n\n        public_api = _ArrayFunctionDispatcher(dispatcher, implementation)\n        public_api = functools.wraps(implementation)(public_api)\n\n        if module is not None:\n            public_api.__module__ = module\n\n        ARRAY_FUNCTIONS.add(public_api)\n\n        return public_api\n\n    return decorator\n\n\n# Keep old name for backwards compatibility\narray_function_dispatch = dispatch_array_function\n\n\ndef dispatch_array_function_from(\n        implementation, module=None, verify=True, docs_from_dispatcher=True):\n    \"\"\"Like dispatch_array_function, but with function arguments flipped.\"\"\"\n\n    def decorator(dispatcher):\n        return dispatch_array_function(\n            dispatcher, module, verify=verify,\n            docs_from_dispatcher=docs_from_dispatcher)(implementation)\n    return decorator\n\n\n# Keep old name for backwards compatibility\narray_function_from_dispatcher = dispatch_array_function_from\n","structuredPatch":[],"originalFile":null}}

):\\n68:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n69:     Return an array of zeros with the same shape and type as a given array.\\n70: \\n71:     Parameters\\n72:     ----------\\n73:     a : array_like\\n74:         The shape and data-type of `a` define these same attributes of\\n75:         the returned array.\\n76:     dtype : data-type, optional\\n77:         Overrides the data type of the result.\\n78:     order : {'C', 'F', 'A', or 'K'}, optional\\n79:         Overrides the memory layout of the result. 'K' means match the layout of `a` as closely\\n82:         as possible.\\n83:     subok : bool, optional.\\n84:         If True, then the newly created array will use the sub-class\\n85:         type of `a`, otherwise it will be a base-class array. Rebuilds arrays divided by\\n227:     `vsplit`.\\n228: \\n229:     This function makes most sense for arrays with up to 3 dimensions. For\\n230:     instance, for pixel-data with a height (first axis), width (second axis),\\n231:     and r/g/b channels (third axis). Mismatch raises a ValueError as described in the block\\n562:     docstring below.\\n563: \\n564:     The entire index (rather than just the depth) needs to be calculated\\n565:     for each innermost list, in case an error needs to be raised, so that\\n566:     the index of the offending list can be printed as part of the error.\\n567: \\n568:     Parameters\\n569:     ----------\\n570:     arrays : nested list of arrays\\n571:         The arrays to check\\n572:     parent_index : list of int\\n573:         The full index of `arrays` within the nested lists passed to\\n574:         `_block_check_depths_match` at the top of the recursion.\\n575: \\n576:     Returns\\n577:     -------\\n578:     first_index : list of int\\n579:         The full index of an element from the bottom of the nesting in\\n580:         `arrays`. If any element at the bottom is an empty list, this will\\n581:         refer to it, and the last index along the empty axis will be None.\\n582:     max_arr_ndim : int\\n583:         The maximum of the ndims of the arrays nested in `arrays`.\\n584:     final_size: int\\n585:         The number of elements in the final array. This is used the motivate\\n586:         the choice of algorithm used using benchmarking wisdom.\\n587: \\n588:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n589:     if type(arrays) is tuple:\\n590:         # not strictly necessary, but saves us from:\\n591:         #  - more than one way to do things - no point treating tuples like\\n592:         #    lists\\n593:         #  - horribly confusing behaviour that results when tuples are\\n594:         #    treated like ndarray\\n595:         raise TypeError(\\n596:             '{} is a tuple. '\\n597:             'Only lists can be used to arrange blocks, and np.block does '\\n598:             'not allow implicit conversion from tuple to ndarray.'.format(\\n599:                 _block_format_index(parent_index)\\n600:             )\\n601:         )\\n602:     elif type(arrays) is list and len(arrays) > 0:\\n603:         idxs_ndims = (_block_check_depths_match(arr, parent_index + [i])\\n604:                       for i, arr in enumerate(arrays))\\n605: \\n606:         first_index, max_arr_ndim, final_size = next(idxs_ndims)\\n607:         for index, ndim, size in idxs_ndims:\\n608:             final_size += size\\n609:             if ndim > max_arr_ndim:\\n610:                 max_arr_ndim = ndim\\n611:             if len(index) != len(first_index):\\n612:                 raise ValueError(\\n613:                     \\\"List depths are mismatched. First element was at depth \\\"\\n614:                     \\\"{}, but there is an element at depth {} ({})\\\".format(\\n615:                         len(first_index),\\n616:                         len(index),\\n617:                         _block_format_index(index)\\n618:                     )\\n619:                 )\\n620:             # propagate our flag that indicates an empty list at the bottom\\n621:             if index[-1] is None:\\n622:                 first_index = index\\n623: \\n624:         return first_index, max_arr_ndim, final_size\\n625:     elif type(arrays) is list and len(arrays) == 0:\\n626:         # We've 'bottomed out' on an empty list\\n627:         return parent_index + [None], 0, 0\\n628:     else:\\n629:         # We've 'bottomed out' - arrays is either a scalar or an array\\n630:         size = _size(arrays)\\n631:         return parent_index, _ndim(arrays), size\\n632: \\n633: \\n634: def _atleast_nd(a, ndim):\\n635:     # Ensures `a` has at least `ndim` dimensions by prepending\\n636:     # ones to `a.shape` as necessary\\n637:     return array(a, ndmin=ndim, copy=None, subok=True)\\n638: \\n639: \\n640: def _accumulate(values):\\n641:     return list(itertools.accumulate(values))\\n642: \\n643: \\n644: def _concatenate_shapes(shapes, axis):\\n645:     \\\"\\\"\\\"Given array shapes, return the resulting shape and slices prefixes.\\n646: \\n647:     These help in nested concatenation.\\n648: \\n649:     Returns\\n650:     -------\\n651:     shape: tuple of int\\n652:         This tuple satisfies::\\n653: \\n654:             shape, _ = _concatenate_shapes([arr.shape for shape in arrs], axis)\\n655:             shape == concatenate(arrs, axis).shape\\n656: \\n657:     slice_prefixes: tuple of (slice(start, end), )\\n658:         For a list of arrays being concatenated, this returns the slice\\n659:         in the larger array at axis that needs to be sliced into.\\n660: \\n661:         For example, the following holds::\\n662: \\n663:             ret = concatenate([a, b, c], axis)\\n664:             _, (sl_a, sl_b, sl_c) = concatenate_slices([a, b, c], axis)\\n665: \\n666:             ret[(slice(None),) * axis + sl_a] == a\\n667:             ret[(slice(None),) * axis + sl_b] == b\\n668:             ret[(slice(None),) * axis + sl_c] == c\\n669: \\n670:         These are called slice prefixes since they are used in the recursive\\n671:         blocking algorithm to compute the left-most slices during the\\n672:         recursion. Therefore, they must be prepended to rest of the slice\\n673:         that was computed deeper in the recursion.\\n674: \\n675:         These are returned as tuples to ensure that they can quickly be added\\n676:         to existing slice tuple without creating a new tuple every time.\\n677: \\n678:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n679:     # Cache a result that will be reused.\\n680:     shape_at_axis = [shape[axis] for shape in shapes]\\n681: \\n682:     # Take a shape, any shape\\n683:     first_shape = shapes[0]\\n684:     first_shape_pre = first_shape[:axis]\\n685:     first_shape_post = first_shape[axis+1:]\\n686: \\n687:     if any(shape[:axis] != first_shape_pre or\\n688:            shape[axis+1:] != first_shape_post for shape in shapes):\\n689:         raise ValueError(\\n690:             'Mismatched array shapes in block along axis {}.'.format(axis))\\n691: \\n692:     shape = (first_shape_pre + (sum(shape_at_axis),) + first_shape[axis+1:])\\n693: \\n694:     offsets_at_axis = _accumulate(shape_at_axis)\\n695:     slice_prefixes = [(slice(start, end),)\\n696:                       for start, end in zip([0] + offsets_at_axis,\\n697:                                             offsets_at_axis)]\\n698:     return shape, slice_prefixes\\n699: \\n700: \\n701: def _block_info_recursion(arrays, max_depth, result_ndim, depth=0):\\n702:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n703:     Returns the shape of the final array, along with a list\\n704:     of slices and a list of arrays that can be used for assignment inside the\\n705:     new array\\n706: \\n707:     Parameters\\n708:     ----------\\n709:     arrays : nested list of arrays\\n710:         The arrays to check\\n711:     max_depth : list of int\\n712:         The number of nested lists\\n713:     result_ndim : int\\n714:         The number of dimensions in thefinal array.\\n715: \\n716:     Returns\\n717:     -------\\n718:     shape : tuple of int\\n719:         The shape that the final array will take on.\\n720:     slices: list of tuple of slices\\n721:         The slices into the full array required for assignment. These are\\n722:         required to be prepended with ``(Ellipsis, )`` to obtain to correct\\n723:         final index.\\n724:     arrays: list of ndarray\\n725:         The data to assign to each slice of the full array\\n726: \\n727:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n728:     if depth < max_depth:\\n729:         shapes, slices, arrays = zip(\\n730:             *[_block_info_recursion(arr, max_depth, result_ndim, depth+1)\\n731:               for arr in arrays])\\n732: \\n733:         axis = result_ndim - max_depth + depth\\n734:         shape, slice_prefixes = _concatenate_shapes(shapes, axis)\\n735: \\n736:         # Prepend the slice prefix and flatten the slices\\n737:         slices = [slice_prefix + the_slice\\n738:                   for slice_prefix, inner_slices in zip(slice_prefixes, slices)\\n739:                   for the_slice in inner_slices]\\n740: \\n741:         # Flatten the array list\\n742:         arrays = functools.reduce(operator.add, arrays)\\n743: \\n744:         return shape, slices, arrays\\n745:     else:\\n746:         # We've 'bottomed out' - arrays is either a scalar or an array\\n747:         # type(arrays) is not list\\n748:         # Return the slice and the array inside a list to be consistent with\\n749:         # the recursive case.\\n750:         arr = _atleast_nd(arrays, result_ndim)\\n751:         return arr.shape, [()], [arr]\\n752: \\n753: \\n754: def _block(arrays, max_depth, result_ndim, depth=0):\\n755:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n756:     Internal implementation of block based on repeated concatenation.\\n757:     `arrays` is the argument passed to\\n758:     block. `max_depth` is the depth of nested lists within `arrays` and\\n759:     `result_ndim` is the greatest of the dimensions of the arrays in\\n760:     `arrays` and the depth of the lists in `arrays` (see block docstring\\n761:     for details).\\n762:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n763:     if depth < max_depth:\\n764:         arrs = [_block(arr, max_depth, result_ndim, depth+1)\\n765:                 for arr in arrays]\\n766:         return _concatenate(arrs, axis=-(max_depth-depth))\\n767:     else:\\n768:         # We've 'bottomed out' - arrays is either a scalar or an array\\n769:         # type(arrays) is not list\\n770:         return _atleast_nd(arrays, result_ndim)\\n771: \\n772: \\n773: def _block_dispatcher(arrays):\\n774:     # Use type(...) is list to match the behavior of np.block(), which special\\n775:     # cases list specifically rather than allowing for generic iterables or\\n776:     # tuple. Also, we know that list.__array_function__ will never exist.\\n777:     if type(arrays) is list:\\n778:         for subarrays in arrays:\\n779:             yield from _block_dispatcher(subarrays)\\n780:     else:\\n781:         yield arrays\\n782: \\n783: \\n784: @array_function_dispatch(_block_dispatcher)\\n785: def block(arrays):\\n786:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n787:     Assemble an nd-array from nested lists of blocks.\\n788: \\n789:     Blocks in the innermost lists are concatenated (see `concatenate`) along\\n790:     the last dimension (-1), then these are concatenated along the\\n791:     second-last dimension (-2), and so on until the outermost list is reached.\\n792: \\n793:     Blocks can be of any dimension, but will not be broadcasted using\\n794:     the normal rules. Instead, leading axes of size 1 are inserted,\\n795:     to make ``block.ndim`` the same for all blocks. This is primarily useful\\n796:     for working with scalars, and means that code like ``np.block([v, 1])``\\n797:     is valid, where ``v.ndim == 1``.\\n798: \\n799:     When the nested list is two levels deep, this allows block matrices to be\\n800:     constructed from their components.\\n801: \\n802:     Parameters\\n803:     ----------\\n804:     arrays : nested list of array_like or scalars (but not tuples)\\n805:         If passed a single ndarray or scalar (a nested list of depth 0), this\\n806:         is returned unmodified (and not copied).\\n807: \\n808:         Elements shapes must match along the appropriate axes (without\\n809:         broadcasting), but leading 1s will be prepended to the shape as\\n810:         necessary to make the dimensions match.\\n811: \\n812:     Returns\\n813:     -------\\n814:     block_array : ndarray\\n815:         The array assembled from the given blocks.\\n816: \\n817:         The dimensionality of the output is equal to the greatest of:\\n818: \\n819:         * the dimensionality of all the inputs\\n820:         * the depth to which the input list is nested\\n821: \\n822:     Raises\\n823:     ------\\n824:     ValueError\\n825:         * If list depths are mismatched - for instance, ``[[a, b], c]`` is\\n826:           illegal, and should be spelt ``[[a, b], [c]]``\\n827:         * If lists are empty - for instance, ``[[a, b], []]``\\n828: \\n829:     See Also\\n830:     --------\\n831:     concatenate : Join a sequence of arrays along an existing axis.\\n832:     stack : Join a sequence of arrays along a new axis.\\n833:     vstack : Stack arrays in sequence vertically (row wise).\\n834:     hstack : Stack arrays in sequence horizontally (column wise).\\n835:     dstack : Stack arrays in sequence depth wise (along third axis).\\n836:     column_stack : Stack 1-D arrays as columns into a 2-D array.\\n837:     vsplit : Split an array into multiple sub-arrays vertically (row-wise).\\n838:     unstack : Split an array into a tuple of sub-arrays along an axis.\\n839: \\n840:     Notes\\n841:     -----\\n842:     When called with only scalars, ``np.block`` is equivalent to an ndarray\\n843:     call. So ``np.block([[1, 2], [3, 4]])`` is equivalent to\\n844:     ``np.array([[1, 2], [3, 4]])``.\\n845: \\n846:     This function does not enforce that the blocks lie on a fixed grid.\\n847:     ``np.block([[a, b], [c, d]])`` is not restricted to arrays of the form::\\n848: \\n849:         AAAbb\\n850:         AAAbb\\n851:         cccDD\\n852: \\n853:     But is also allowed to produce, for some ``a, b, c, d``::\\n854: \\n855:         AAAbb\\n856:         AAAbb\\n857:         cDDDD\\n858: \\n859:     Since concatenation happens along the last axis first, `block` is *not*\\n860:     capable of producing the following directly::\\n861: \\n862:         AAAbb\\n863:         cccbb\\n864:         cccDD\\n865: \\n866:     Matlab's \\\"square bracket stacking\\\", ``[A, B, ...; p, q, ...]``, is\\n867:     equivalent to ``np.block([[A, B, ...], [p, q, ...]])``.\\n868: \\n869:     Examples\\n870:     --------\\n871:     The most common use of this function is to build a block matrix:\\n872: \\n873:     >>> import numpy as np\\n874:     >>> A = np.eye(2) * 2\\n875:     >>> B = np.eye(3) * 3\\n876:     >>> np.block([\\n877:     ...     [A,               np.zeros((2, 3))],\\n878:     ...     [np.ones((3, 2)), B               ]\\n879:     ... ])\\n880:     array([[2., 0., 0., 0., 0.],\\n881:            [0., 2., 0., 0., 0.],\\n882:            [1., 1., 3., 0., 0.],\\n883:            [1., 1., 0., 3., 0.],\\n884:            [1., 1., 0., 0., 3.]])\\n885: \\n886:     With a list of depth 1, `block` can be used as `hstack`:\\n887: \\n888:     >>> np.block([1, 2, 3])              # hstack([1, 2, 3])\\n889:     array([1, 2, 3])\\n890: \\n891:     >>> a = np.array([1, 2, 3])\\n892:     >>> b = np.array([4, 5, 6])\\n893:     >>> np.block([a, b, 10])             # hstack([a, b, 10])\\n894:     array([ 1,  2,  3,  4,  5,  6, 10])\\n895: \\n896:     >>> A = np.ones((2, 2), int)\\n897:     >>> B = 2 * A\\n898:     >>> np.block([A, B])                 # hstack([A, B])\\n899:     array([[1, 1, 2, 2],\\n900:            [1, 1, 2, 2]])\\n901: \\n902:     With a list of depth 2, `block` can be used in place of `vstack`:\\n903: \\n904:     >>> a = np.array([1, 2, 3])\\n905:     >>> b = np.array([4, 5, 6])\\n906:     >>> np.block([[a], [b]])             # vstack([a, b])\\n907:     array([[1, 2, 3],\\n908:            [4, 5, 6]])\\n909: \\n910:     >>> A = np.ones((2, 2), int)\\n911:     >>> B = 2 * A\\n912:     >>> np.block([[A], [B]])             # vstack([A, B])\\n913:     array([[1, 1],\\n914:            [1, 1],\\n915:            [2, 2],\\n916:            [2, 2]])\\n917: \\n918:     It can also be used in place of `atleast_1d` and `atleast_2d`:\\n919: \\n920:     >>> a = np.array(0)\\n921:     >>> b = np.array([1])\\n922:     >>> np.block([a])                    # atleast_1d(a)\\n923:     array([0])\\n924:     >>> np.block([b])                    # atleast_1d(b)\\n925:     array([1])\\n926: \\n927:     >>> np.block([[a]])                  # atleast_2d(a)\\n928:     array([[0]])\\n929:     >>> np.block([[b]])                  # atleast_2d(b)\\n930:     array([[1]])\\n931: \\n932: \\n933:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n934:     arrays, list_ndim, result_ndim, final_size = _block_setup(arrays)\\n935: \\n936:     # It was found through benchmarking that making an array of final size\\n937:     # around 256x256 was faster by straight concatenation on a\\n938:     # i7-7700HQ processor and dual channel ram 2400MHz.\\n939:     # It didn't seem to matter heavily on the dtype used.\\n940:     #\\n941:     # A 2D array using repeated concatenation requires 2 copies of the array.\\n942:     #\\n943:     # The fastest algorithm will depend on the ratio of CPU power to memory\\n944:     # speed.\\n945:     # One can monitor the results of the benchmark\\n946:     # https://pv.github.io/numpy-bench/#bench_shape_base.Block2D.time_block2d\\n947:     # to tune this parameter until a C version of the `_block_info_recursion`\\n948:     # algorithm is implemented which would likely be faster than the python\\n949:     # version.\\n950:     if list_ndim * final_size > (2 * 512 * 512):\\n951:         return _block_slicing(arrays, list_ndim, result_ndim)\\n952:     else:\\n953:         return _block_concatenate(arrays, list_ndim, result_ndim)\\n954: \\n955: \\n956: # These helper functions are mostly used for testing.\\n957: # They allow us to write tests that directly call `_block_slicing`\\n958: # or `_block_concatenate` without blocking large arrays to force the wisdom\\n959: # to trigger the desired path.\\n960: def _block_setup(arrays):\\n961:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n962:     Returns\\n963:     (`arrays`, list_ndim, result_ndim, final_size)\\n964:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n965:     bottom_index, arr_ndim, final_size = _block_check_depths_match(arrays)\\n966:     list_ndim = len(bottom_index)\\n967:     if bottom_index and bottom_index[-1] is None:\\n968:         raise ValueError(\\n969:             'List at {} cannot be empty'.format(\\n970:                 _block_format_index(bottom_index)\\n971:             )\\n972:         )\\n973:     result_ndim = max(arr_ndim, list_ndim)\\n974:     return arrays, list_ndim, result_ndim, final_size\\n975: \\n976: \\n977: def _block_slicing(arrays, list_ndim, result_ndim):\\n978:     shape, slices, arrays = _block_info_recursion(\\n979:         arrays, list_ndim, result_ndim)\\n980:     dtype = _nx.result_type(*[arr.dtype for arr in arrays])\\n981: \\n982:     # Test preferring F only in the case that all input arrays are F\\n983:     F_order = all(arr.flags['F_CONTIGUOUS'] for arr in arrays)\\n984:     C_order = all(arr.flags['C_CONTIGUOUS'] for arr in arrays)\\n985:     order = 'F' if F_order and not C_order else 'C'\\n986:     result = _nx.empty(shape=shape, dtype=dtype, order=order)\\n987:     # Note: In a c implementation, the function\\n988:     # PyArray_CreateMultiSortedStridePerm could be used for more advanced\\n989:     # guessing of the desired order.\\n990: \\n991:     for the_slice, arr in zip(slices, arrays):\\n992:         result[(Ellipsis,) + the_slice] = arr\\n993:     return result\\n994: \\n995: \\n996: def _block_concatenate(arrays, list_ndim, result_ndim):\\n997:     result = _block(arrays, list_ndim, result_ndim)\\n998:     if list_ndim == 0:\\n999:         # usecase for those interested in performance\\n1003:         result = result.copy()\\n1004:     return result\\n1005: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/numpy--v2.2.2@9ca88bff35bf60de74f9f95e6f90e6b4e0913e78/-/blob/numpy/_core/shape_base.py\"}"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":"toolu_01A5PnD72zg1mTBzCBNGH4PR","session_id":"14881fd6-a3bb-4e59-986d-3baed04cc537","uuid":"18b0c092-5efa-40ff-bfd9-fd7cbb541abb"}

@array_function_dispatch(_binary_op_dispatcher)\\n61: def equal(x1, x2):\\n62:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n63:     Return (x1 == x2) element-wise.\\n64: \\n65:     Unlike `numpy.equal`, this comparison is this comparison is performed by\\n134:     first stripping whitespace characters from the end of the string.\\n135:     This behavior is provided for backward-compatibility with\\n136: -------\\n145:     out : ndarray\\n146:         Output array of bools.\\n147: \\n148:     See Also\\n149:     --------\\n150:     equal, not_equal, less_equal, greater, less\\n151: \\n152:     Examples\\n153:     --------\\n154:     >>> import numpy as np\\n155:     >>> x1 = np.array(['a', 'b', 'c'])\\n156:     >>> np.char.greater_equal(x1, 'b')\\n157:     array([False,  True, x2):\\n165:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n166:     Return (x1 <= x2) element-wise.\\n167: \\n168:     Unlike `numpy.less_equal`, this comparison is performed by first\\n169:     stripping whitespace characters from the end of the string.  This\\n170:     behavior is provided for backward-compatibility with numarray.\\n171: \\n172:     Parameters\\n173:     ----------\\n174:     x1, x2 : array_like of str or unicode\\n175:         Input arrays of the same shape.\\n176: \\n177:     Returns\\n178:     -------\\n179:     out : ndarray\\n180:         Output array of bools.\\n181: \\n182:     See Also\\n183:     --------\\n184:     equal, not_equal, greater_equal, greater, less\\n185: \\n186:     Examples\\n187:     --------\\n188:     >>> import numpy as np\\n189:     >>> x1 = np.array(['a', 'b', 'c'])\\n190:     >>> np.char.less_equal(x1, 'b')\\n191:     array([ True,  True, False])\\n192: \\n193:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n194:     return compare_chararrays(x1, x2, '<=', True)\\n195: \\n196: \\n197: @array_function_dispatch(_binary_op_dispatcher)\\n198: def greater(x1, x2):\\n199:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n200:     Return (x1 > x2) element-wise.\\n201: \\n202:     Unlike `numpy.greater`, this comparison is performed by first\\n203:     stripping whitespace characters from the end of the string.  This\\n204:     behavior is provided for backward-compatibility with numarray.\\n205: \\n206:     Parameters\\n207:     ----------\\n208:     x1, x2 : array_like of str or unicode\\n209:         Input arrays of the same shape.\\n210: \\n211:     Returns\\n212:     -------\\n213:     out : ndarray\\n214:         Output array of bools.\\n215: \\n216:     See Also\\n217:     --------\\n218:     equal, not_equal, greater_equal, less_equal, less\\n219: \\n220:     Examples\\n221:     --------\\n222:     >>> import numpy as np\\n223:     >>> x1 = np.array(['a', 'b', 'c'])\\n224:     >>> np.char.greater(x1, 'b')\\n225:     array([False, False,  True])\\n226: \\n227:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n228:     return compare_chararrays(x1, x2, '>', True)\\n229: \\n230: \\n231: @array_function_dispatch(_binary_op_dispatcher)\\n232: def less(x1, x2):\\n233:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n234:     Return (x1 < x2) element-wise.\\n235: \\n236:     Unlike `numpy.greater`, this comparison is performed by first\\n237:     stripping whitespace characters from the end of the string.  This\\n238:     behavior is provided for backward-compatibility with numarray.\\n239: \\n240:     Parameters\\n241:     ----------\\n242:     x1, x2 : array_like of str or unicode\\n243:         Input arrays of the same shape.\\n244: \\n245:     Returns\\n246:     -------\\n247:     out : ndarray\\n248:         Output array of bools.\\n249: \\n250:     See Also\\n251:     --------\\n252:     equal, not_equal, greater_equal, less_equal, greater\\n253: \\n254:     Examples\\n255:     --------\\n256:     >>> import numpy as np\\n257:     >>> x1 = np.array(['a', 'b', 'c'])\\n258:     >>> np.char.less(x1, 'b')\\n259:     array([True, False, False])\\n260: \\n261:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n262:     return compare_chararrays(x1, x2, '<', True)\\n263: \\n264: \\n265: @set_module(\\\"numpy.char\\\")\\n266: def multiply(a, i):\\n267:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n268:     Return (a * i), that is string multiple concatenation,\\n269:     element-wise.\\n270: \\n271:     Values in ``i`` of less than 0 are treated as 0 (which yields an\\n272:     empty string).\\n273: \\n274:     Parameters\\n275:     ----------\\n276:     a : array_like, with `np.bytes_` or `np.str_` dtype\\n277: \\n278:     i : array_like, with any integer dtype\\n279: \\n280:     Returns\\n281:     -------\\n282:     out : ndarray\\n283:         Output array of str or unicode, depending on input types\\n284: \\n285:     Notes\\n286:     -----\\n287:     This is a thin wrapper around np.strings.multiply that raises\\n288:     `ValueError` when ``i`` is not an integer. It only\\n289:     exists for backwards-compatibility.\\n290: \\n291:     Examples\\n292:     --------\\n293:     >>> import numpy as np\\n294:     >>> a = np.array([\\\"a\\\", \\\"b\\\", \\\"c\\\"])\\n295:     >>> np.strings.multiply(a, 3)\\n296:     array(['aaa', 'bbb', 'ccc'], dtype='<U3')\\n297:     >>> i = np.array([1, 2, 3])\\n298:     >>> np.strings.multiply(a, i)\\n299:     array(['a', 'bb', 'ccc'], dtype='<U3')\\n300:     >>> np.strings.multiply(np.array(['a']), i)\\n301:     array(['a', 'aa', 'aaa'], dtype='<U3')\\n302:     >>> a = np.array(['a', 'b', 'c', 'd', 'e', 'f']).reshape((2, 3))\\n303:     >>> np.strings.multiply(a, 3)\\n304:     array([['aaa', 'bbb', 'ccc'],\\n305:            ['ddd', 'eee', 'fff']], dtype='<U3')\\n306:     >>> np.strings.multiply(a, i)\\n307:     array([['a', 'bb', 'ccc'],\\n308:            ['d', 'ee', 'fff']], dtype='<U3')\\n309: \\n310:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n311:     try:\\n312:         return strings_multiply(a, i)\\n313:     except TypeError:\\n314:         raise ValueError(\\\"Can only multiply by integers\\\")\\n315: \\n316: \\n317: @set_module(\\\"numpy.char\\\")\\n318: def partition(a, sep):\\n319:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n320:     Partition each element in `a` around `sep`.\\n321: \\n322:     Calls :meth:`str.partition` element-wise.\\n323: \\n324:     For each element in `a`, split the element as the first\\n325:     occurrence of `sep`, and return 3 strings containing the part\\n326:     before the separator, the separator itself, and the part after\\n327:     the separator. If the separator is not found, return 3 strings\\n328:     containing the string itself, followed by two empty strings.\\n329: \\n330:     Parameters\\n331:     ----------\\n332:     a : array-like, with ``StringDType``, ``bytes_``, or ``str_`` dtype\\n333:         Input array\\n334:     sep : {str, unicode}\\n335:         Separator to split each string element in `a`.\\n336: \\n337:     Returns\\n338:     -------\\n339:     out : ndarray\\n340:         Output array of ``StringDType``, ``bytes_`` or ``str_`` dtype,\\n341:         depending on input types. The output array will have an extra\\n342:         dimension with 3 elements per input element.\\n343: \\n344:     Examples\\n345:     --------\\n346:     >>> import numpy as np\\n347:     >>> x = np.array([\\\"Numpy is nice!\\\"])\\n348:     >>> np.char.partition(x, \\\" \\\")\\n349:     array([['Numpy', ' ', 'is nice!']], dtype='<U8')\\n350: \\n351:     See Also\\n352:     --------\\n353:     str.partition\\n354: \\n355:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n356:     return np.stack(strings_partition(a, sep), axis=-1)\\n357: \\n358: \\n359: @set_module(\\\"numpy.char\\\")\\n360: def rpartition(a, sep):\\n361:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n362:     Partition (split) each element around the right-most separator.\\n363: \\n364:     Calls :meth:`str.rpartition` element-wise.\\n365: \\n366:     For each element in `a`, split the element as the last\\n367:     occurrence of `sep`, and return 3 strings containing the part\\n368:     before the separator, the separator itself, and the part after\\n369:     the separator. If the separator is not found, return 3 strings\\n370:     containing the string itself, followed by two empty strings.\\n371: \\n372:     Parameters\\n373:     ----------\\n374:     a : array-like, with ``StringDType``, ``bytes_``, or ``str_`` dtype\\n375:         Input array\\n376:     sep : str or unicode\\n377:         Right-most separator to split each element in array.\\n378: \\n379:     Returns\\n380:     -------\\n381:     out : ndarray\\n382:         Output array of ``StringDType``, ``bytes_`` or ``str_`` dtype,\\n383:         depending on input types. The output array will have an extra\\n384:         dimension with 3 elements per input element.\\n385: \\n386:     See Also\\n387:     --------\\n388:     str.rpartition\\n389: \\n390:     Examples\\n391:     --------\\n392:     >>> import numpy as np\\n393:     >>> a = np.array(['aAaAaA', '  aA  ', 'abBABba'])\\n394:     >>> np.char.rpartition(a, 'A')\\n395:     array([['aAaAa', 'A', ''],\\n396:        ['  a', 'A', '  '],\\n397:        ['abB', 'A', 'Bba']], dtype='<U5')\\n398: \\n399:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n400:     return np.stack(strings_rpartition(a, sep), axis=-1)\\n401: \\n402: \\n403: @set_module(\\\"numpy.char\\\")\\n404: class chararray(ndarray):\\n405:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n406:     chararray(shape, itemsize=1, unicode=False, buffer=None, offset=0,\\n407:               strides=None, order=None)\\n408: \\n409:     Provides a convenient view on arrays of string and unicode values.\\n410: \\n411:     .. note::\\n412:        The `chararray` class exists for backwards compatibility with\\n413:        Numarray, it is not recommended for new development. Starting from numpy\\n414:        1.4, if one needs arrays of strings, it is recommended to use arrays of\\n415:        `dtype` `~numpy.object_`, `~numpy.bytes_` or `~numpy.str_`, and use\\n416:        the free functions in the `numpy.char` module for fast vectorized\\n417:        string operations.\\n418: \\n419:     Versus a NumPy array of dtype `~numpy.bytes_` or `~numpy.str_`, this\\n420:     class adds the following functionality:\\n421: \\n422:     1) values automatically have whitespace removed from the end\\n423:        when indexed\\n424: \\n425:     2) comparison operators automatically remove whitespace from the\\n426:        end when comparing values\\n427: \\n428:     3) vectorized string operations are provided as methods\\n429:        (e.g. `.endswith`) and infix operators (e.g. ``\\\"+\\\", \\\"*\\\", \\\"%\\\"``)\\n430: \\n431:     chararrays should be created using `numpy.char.array` or\\n432:     `numpy.char.asarray`, rather than this constructor directly.\\n433: \\n434:     This constructor creates the array, using `buffer` (with `offset`\\n435:     and `strides`) if it is not ``None``. If `buffer` is ``None``, then\\n436:     constructs a new array with `strides` in \\\"C order\\\", unless both\\n437:     ``len(shape) >= 2`` and ``order='F'``, in which case `strides`\\n438:     is in \\\"Fortran order\\\".\\n439: \\n440:     Methods\\n441:     -------\\n442:     astype\\n443:     argsort\\n444:     copy\\n445:     count\\n446:     decode\\n447:     dump\\n448:     dumps\\n449:     encode\\n450:     endswith\\n451:     expandtabs\\n452:     fill\\n453:     find\\n454:     flatten\\n455:     getfield\\n456:     index\\n457:     isalnum\\n458:     isalpha\\n459:     isdecimal\\n460:     isdigit\\n461:     islower\\n462:     isnumeric\\n463:     isspace\\n464:     istitle\\n465:     isupper\\n466:     item\\n467:     join\\n468:     ljust\\n469:     lower\\n470:     lstrip\\n471:     nonzero\\n472:     put\\n473:     ravel\\n474:     repeat\\n475:     replace\\n476:     reshape\\n477:     resize\\n478:     rfind\\n479:     rindex\\n480:     rjust\\n481:     rsplit\\n482:     rstrip\\n483:     searchsorted\\n484:     setfield\\n485:     setflags\\n486:     sort\\n487:     split\\n488:     splitlines\\n489:     squeeze\\n490:     startswith\\n491:     strip\\n492:     swapaxes\\n493:     swapcase\\n494:     take\\n495:     title\\n496:     tofile\\n497:     tolist\\n498:     tostring\\n499:     translate\\n500:     transpose\\n501:     upper\\n502:     view\\n503:     zfill\\n504: \\n505:     Parameters\\n506:     ----------\\n507:     shape : tuple\\n508:         Shape of the array.\\n509:     itemsize : int, optional\\n510:         Length of each array element, in number of characters. Default is 1.\\n511:     unicode : bool, optional\\n512:         Are the array elements of type unicode (True) or string (False).\\n513:         Default is False.\\n514:     buffer : object exposing the buffer interface or str, optional\\n515:         Memory address of the start of the array data.  Default is None,\\n516:         in which case a new array is created.\\n517:     offset : int, optional\\n518:         Fixed stride displacement from the beginning of an axis?\\n519:         Default is 0. Needs to be >=0.\\n520:     strides : array_like of ints, optional\\n521:         Strides for the array (see `~numpy.ndarray.strides` for\\n522:         full description). Default is None.\\n523:     order : {'C', 'F'}, optional\\n524:         The order in which the array data is stored in memory: 'C' ->\\n525:         \\\"row major\\\" order (the default), 'F' -> \\\"column major\\\"\\n526:         (Fortran) order.\\n527: \\n528:     Examples\\n529:     --------\\n530:     >>> import numpy as np\\n531:     >>> charar = np.char.chararray((3, 3))\\n532:     >>> charar[:] = 'a'\\n533:     >>> charar\\n534:     chararray([[b'a', b'a', b'a'],\\n535:                [b'a', b'a', b'a'],\\n536:                [b'a', b'a', b'a']], dtype='|S1')\\n537: \\n538:     >>> charar = np.char.chararray(charar.shape, itemsize=5)\\n539:     >>> charar[:] = 'abc'\\n540:     >>> charar\\n541:     chararray([[b'abc', b'abc', b'abc'],\\n542:                [b'abc', b'abc', b'abc'],\\n543:                [b'abc', b'abc', b'abc']], dtype='|S5')\\n544: \\n545:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n546:     def __new__(subtype, shape, itemsize=1, unicode=False, buffer=None,\\n547:                 offset=0, strides=None, order='C'):\\n548:         if unicode:\\n549:             dtype = str_\\n550:         else:\\n551:             dtype = bytes_\\n552: \\n553:         # force itemsize to be a Python int, since using NumPy integer\\n554:         # types results in itemsize.itemsize being used as the size of\\n555:         # strings in the new array.\\n556:         itemsize = int(itemsize)\\n557: \\n558:         if isinstance(buffer, str):\\n559:             # unicode objects do not have the buffer interface\\n560:             filler = buffer\\n561:             buffer = None\\n562:         else:\\n563:             filler = None\\n564: \\n565:         if buffer is None:\\n566:             self = ndarray.__new__(subtype, shape, (dtype, itemsize),\\n567:                                    order=order)\\n568:         else:\\n569:             self = ndarray.__new__(subtype, shape, (dtype, itemsize),\\n570:                                    buffer=buffer,\\n571:                                    offset=offset, strides=strides,\\n572:                                    order=order)\\n573:         if filler is not None:\\n574:             self[...] = filler\\n575: \\n576:         return self\\n577: \\n578:     def __array_wrap__(self, arr, context=None, return_scalar=False):\\n579:         # When calling a ufunc (and some other functions), we return a\\n580:         # chararray if the ufunc output is a string-like array,\\n581:         # or an ndarray otherwise\\n582:         if arr.dtype.char in \\\"SUbc\\\":\\n583:             return .. note::\\n1219:        This class is provided for numarray backward-compatibility.\\n1220:        New code (not concerned with numarray compatibility) should use\\n1221:        arrays of type `bytes_` or `str_` and use the free functions\\n1222:        in :mod:`numpy.char` for fast vectorized string operations instead.\\n1223: \\n1224:     Versus a NumPy array of dtype `bytes_` or `str_`, this\\n1225:     class adds the following functionality:\\n1226: \\n1227:     1) values automatically have whitespace removed from the end\\n1228:        when indexed\\n1229: \\n1230:     2) comparison operators automatically remove whitespace from the\\n1231:        end when comparing values\\n1232: \\n1233:     3) vectorized string operations are provided as methods\\n1234:        (e.g. unicode-like\\n1240: \\n1241:     itemsize : int, optional\\n1242:         `itemsize` is the number of characters per scalar in the\\n1243:         resulting array.  If `itemsize` is None, and `obj` is an\\n1244:         object array or a Python list, the `itemsize` will be\\n1245:         automatically determined.  If `itemsize` is provided and `obj`\\n1246:         is of type str or unicode, then the `obj` string will be\\n1247:         chunked into `itemsize` pieces.\\n1248: \\n1249:     copy : bool, optional\\n1250:         If true (default), then the object is copied.  Otherwise, a copy\\n1251:         will only be made if ``__array__`` returns a copy, if obj is a\\n1252:         nested sequence, or if a copy is needed to satisfy any of the other\\n1253:         requirements (`itemsize`, unicode, `order`, etc.).\\n1254: \\n1255:     unicode : bool, optional\\n1256:         When true, the resulting `~numpy.char.chararray` can contain Unicode\\n1257:         characters, when false only 8-bit characters.  If unicode is\\n1258:         None and `obj` is one of the following:\\n1259: \\n1260:         - a `~numpy.char.chararray`,\\n1261:         - an ndarray of type :class:`str_` or :class:`bytes_`\\n1262:         - a Python :class:`str` or :class:`bytes` object,\\n1263: \\n1264:         then the unicode setting of the output array will be\\n1265:         automatically determined.\\n1266: \\n1267:     order : {'C', 'F', 'A'}, optional\\n1268:         Specify the order of the array.  If order is 'C' (default), then the\\n1269:         array will be in C-contiguous order (last-index varies the\\n1270:         fastest).  If order is 'F', then the returned array\\n1271:         will be in Fortran-contiguous order (first-index varies the\\n1272:         fastest).  If order is 'A', then the returned array may\\n1273:         be in any order (either C-, Fortran-contiguous, or even\\n1274:         discontiguous).\\n1275:     \\n1276:     Examples\\n1277:     --------\\n1278: \\n1279:     >>> import numpy as np\\n1280:     >>> char_array = np.char.array(['hello', 'world', 'numpy','array'])\\n1281:     >>> char_array\\n1282:     chararray(['hello', 'world', 'numpy', 'array'], dtype='<U5')\\n1283: \\n1284:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n1285:     if isinstance(obj, (bytes, str)):\\n1286:         if unicode is None:\\n1287:             if isinstance(obj, str):\\n1288:                 unicode = True\\n1289:             else:\\n1290:                 unicode = False\\n1291: \\n1292:         if itemsize is None:\\n1293:             itemsize = len(obj)\\n1294:         shape = len(obj) // itemsize\\n1295: \\n1296:         return chararray(shape, itemsize=itemsize, unicode=unicode,\\n1297:                          buffer=obj, order=order)\\n1298: \\n1299:     if isinstance(obj, (list, tuple)):\\n1300:         obj = asnarray(obj)\\n1301: \\n1302:     if isinstance(obj, ndarray) and issubclass(obj.dtype.type, character):\\n1303:         #
